<div id="review_798702243" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/798702243" />
    <a title="Greg Brozeit" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26552534-greg-brozeit"><img alt="Greg Brozeit" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1544324486p2/26552534.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/798702243?book_show_action=true">Dec 24, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Greg Brozeit" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Greg Brozeit" href="/user/show/26552534-greg-brozeit">Greg Brozeit</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title=" 9780450003400" href="/book/show/863966.Junky">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26552534-greg-brozeit?shelf=american-fiction">american-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26552534-greg-brozeit?shelf=no-la">no-la</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer798702243" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer793970982884362324">Reading <i>Junky</i> as a college student was a revelation: it was the first time I felt I heard an author’s authentic voice. Burroughs’s clipped sentences, his directness, his matter-of-fact statements about what things were really like, his view of a world I didn’t know about began a life-long fascination. It’s easy to dismiss <i>Junky</i> because of it’s subject matter of heroin addiction; that it’s just a fad or something young adults might think is cool. But he does it with such artistic depth. Even the </span>
  <span id="freeText793970982884362324" style="display:none">Reading <i>Junky</i> as a college student was a revelation: it was the first time I felt I heard an author’s authentic voice. Burroughs’s clipped sentences, his directness, his matter-of-fact statements about what things were really like, his view of a world I didn’t know about began a life-long fascination. It’s easy to dismiss <i>Junky</i> because of it’s subject matter of heroin addiction; that it’s just a fad or something young adults might think is cool. But he does it with such artistic depth. Even the simplest sentences echo something profound.<br /><br /><i>Junky</i> also has a timelessness. The historical setting is more than 60 years old, but it still seems and sounds contemporary. Indeed, although it was published in 1953, his description of New Orleans is still the most concise, accurate description I’ve ever read and it applies today:<br /><blockquote>There are people in New Orleans who have never been outside the city limits. The New Orleans accent is exactly similar to the accent of Brooklyn. The French Quarter is always crowded. Tourists, servicemen, merchant seamen, gamblers, perverts, drifters, and lamsters from every State in the Union. People wander around, unrelated, purposeless, most of them looking vaguely sullen and hostile. This is a place where you enjoy yourself. Even the criminals have to come here to cool off and relax.<br /><br />But a complex pattern of tensions, like the electrical mazes devised by psychologists to unhinge the nervous systems of white rats and guinea pigs, keeps the unhappy pleasure-seekers in a condition of unconsummated alertness. For one thing, New Orleans in inordinately noisy. The drivers orient themselves largely by the use of their horns, like bats. The residents are surly. The transient population is completely miscellaneous and unrelated, so that you never know what sort of behavior to expect from anybody.</blockquote><br />I think reading that motivated me to pick up and read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/788408369" rel="nofollow noopener"> <i>A Confederacy of Dunces<i></i></i></a> immediately after finishing. And after that I read Gabriel Garcia Marquez’s <i>One Hundred Years of Solitude</i>. It was truly the month in which I learned the true joy of reading.<br /><br />Yesterday I was in the car for three hours and finally got to listen to the entire reading of <i>Junky</i> by Burroughs himself. His <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=DnxweVAvE5w" rel="nofollow noopener">real voice</a> was even more compelling than his written voice. His voice could fit in with the work of <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=aia3b1AFcTU" rel="nofollow noopener">hip-hop</a> artists, <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=3ajahePtEaY" rel="nofollow noopener">conceptual musicians</a> (oh this piece is so wonderful), and <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=KMbx1f43Y9A" rel="nofollow noopener">reinterpret</a> rock classics to great effect.<br /><br />Despite the often grim and gritty reality of heroin addiction, <i>Junky</i> never fails to remind me why I love to read and inspires me to keep reading outside of my comfort zone.</span>
  <a data-text-id="793970982884362324" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_129501683" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/129501683" />
    <a title="Erik Graff" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/974210-erik-graff"><img alt="Erik Graff" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1299888977p2/974210.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/129501683?book_show_action=true">Nov 06, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Erik Graff" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Erik Graff" href="/user/show/974210-erik-graff">Erik Graff</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback " href="/book/show/1447116.Junkie">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Burroughs fans</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Erik by: </span>
      no one
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/974210-erik-graff?shelf=literature">literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer129501683" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer157420507203208258">Other than smoking some in high school and reading some books taking either a sociological or pharmacological approach to the subject, I've never had much exposure to the heroin habit. The pleasure it afforded during a week of adolescent experimentation wasn't captivating and although I've been acquainted with some habitual users, I've never been intimate with one, never lived with one. This semi-autobiographical account is the closest I've ever gotten to how it might feel to be a person with th</span>
  <span id="freeText157420507203208258" style="display:none">Other than smoking some in high school and reading some books taking either a sociological or pharmacological approach to the subject, I've never had much exposure to the heroin habit. The pleasure it afforded during a week of adolescent experimentation wasn't captivating and although I've been acquainted with some habitual users, I've never been intimate with one, never lived with one. This semi-autobiographical account is the closest I've ever gotten to how it might feel to be a person with the habit and no ever-ready way to sustain it. It's a dark picture.<br /><br />Habits I do understand. I myself am a smoker and have been in circumstances of desire where I've walked considerable distances at night to buy a pack. I've also tried fasting and have noted that the expectation of eating every day has a similar insistency. Also, I've known, even lived with, persons with serious alcohol habits. All share common characteristics, but all are different enough that it takes something like Burroughs' book for me to begin to appreciate heroin.<br /><br />The most affecting parts of 'Junkie' are those describing the gulf between the obsessively felt need and its satisfaction. The protagonist is no rich man able to manage his habit safely and get on with the rest of life. Instead, he must hustle; must risk running afoul of the law; may, in extremis, betray any relationship, break any convention. He's depraved and he knows it--a condition only mitigated by the fact that many of his "friends" and associates are in a similar situation.<br /><br />Burroughs was one of the popular writers among my high school set. I avoided him because the descriptions I'd heard of his 'Naked Lunch' and other books made them sound confusing and obscure and because, frankly, I had a strong prejudice against heroin and heroin addicts. Over the years, however, reading more about him and some extracts of his writing in other books, I softened my opposition enough to pick this one up when I came upon it. It certainly wasn't fun, but it was a quick and dirty read and would likely be of service to anyone at risk or concerned about heroin users. I passed on my copy immediately.</span>
  <a data-text-id="157420507203208258" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_98112051" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/98112051" />

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/98112051?book_show_action=true">Apr 12, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matthew W" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matthew W" href="/user/show/2183563-matthew-w">Matthew W</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer98112051" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10076669062979091253">The title "Junky" says it all, William S. Burroughs' virtual junky diary is a trip through the author's own self-inflicted and self-injected personal hell. If public schools really wanted kids to abstain from injecting needles in their arms they should drop D.A.R.E. and force students to read William S. Burroughs's "Junky." I don't think I have ever read another book (including autopsy and death scene photo books) that has nauseated the way this book has and that includes all the other novels I </span>
  <span id="freeText10076669062979091253" style="display:none">The title "Junky" says it all, William S. Burroughs' virtual junky diary is a trip through the author's own self-inflicted and self-injected personal hell. If public schools really wanted kids to abstain from injecting needles in their arms they should drop D.A.R.E. and force students to read William S. Burroughs's "Junky." I don't think I have ever read another book (including autopsy and death scene photo books) that has nauseated the way this book has and that includes all the other novels I have read written by WSB. The difference between "Junky" and all of WSB's other novels (aside from "Queer") is that "Junky" is a book based on real-life personal experience whereas the others are for the most part deranged cut-up dreamworlds. <br /><br />You will feel a little dirtier after reading "Junky."</span>
  <a data-text-id="10076669062979091253" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review98112051">
  <td colspan="2">
    <div class="updateActionLinks">
        <a class="right flag" rel="nofollow" id="flag_link98112051" title="Flag this review as inappropriate." href="/flagged/new?resource_id=98112051&amp;resource_type=Review&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F23940.Junky">flag</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer15752484992696803380"><br/>I want to point out that my low rating for this one is based from a perspective of someone who, unfortunately, grew up around a lot of junkies. I can only guess that for those who have not experienced it, this story could be poignant, shocking even. As for me, not only was there nothing disturbing about this book for me, but there just seemed to be no story here. It felt like reading the journal of an addict who daily described how he did his drugs, how he scored, and the prices he paid for his </span>
  <span id="freeText15752484992696803380" style="display:none"><br />I want to point out that my low rating for this one is based from a perspective of someone who, unfortunately, grew up around a lot of junkies. I can only guess that for those who have not experienced it, this story could be poignant, shocking even. As for me, not only was there nothing disturbing about this book for me, but there just seemed to be no story here. It felt like reading the journal of an addict who daily described how he did his drugs, how he scored, and the prices he paid for his actions. <br />Watching "Judge Judy" would be more entertaining, and I don't watch "Judge Judy". </span>

<div id="review_1385097464" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1385097464" />
    <a title="sologdin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5610133-sologdin"><img alt="sologdin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1307928658p2/5610133.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1385097464?book_show_action=true">Sep 06, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="sologdin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="sologdin" href="/user/show/5610133-sologdin">sologdin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5610133-sologdin?shelf=of-best-sentence-and-moost-solaas">of-best-sentence-and-moost-solaas</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5610133-sologdin?shelf=orleans-nouvelle">orleans-nouvelle</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1385097464" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1609022385892597672">After reading this, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/135836.Trainspotting' title='Trainspotting by Irvine Welsh' rel='nofollow noopener'> Trainspotting</a>, and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/46945.Requiem_for_a_Dream' title='Requiem for a Dream by Hubert Selby Jr.' rel='nofollow noopener'> Requiem for a Dream</a>, have decided that injecting heroin is unambiguously awful. (Text is unequivocal that junk is “the worst thing that can happen to a man” (8).)<br/><br/>Non-fictive outworks proclaims that it takes “at least three months’ shooting twice a day to get any habit at all […] no exaggeration to say it takes about a year and several hundred injections to make an addict” (xv).<br/><br/>Addiction rewrites the corporeal constitution: “when you stop growing you start</span>
  <span id="freeText1609022385892597672" style="display:none">After reading this, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/135836.Trainspotting" title="Trainspotting by Irvine Welsh" rel="nofollow noopener"> Trainspotting</a>, and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/46945.Requiem_for_a_Dream" title="Requiem for a Dream by Hubert Selby Jr." rel="nofollow noopener"> Requiem for a Dream</a>, have decided that injecting heroin is unambiguously awful. (Text is unequivocal that junk is “the worst thing that can happen to a man” (8).)<br /><br />Non-fictive outworks proclaims that it takes “at least three months’ shooting twice a day to get any habit at all […] no exaggeration to say it takes about a year and several hundred injections to make an addict” (xv).<br /><br />Addiction rewrites the corporeal constitution: “when you stop growing you start dying. An addict never stops growing. Most users periodically kick the habit, which involves shrinking the organism and replacement of the junk-dependent cells” (xv). Heroin is significantly not the exception, but is rather the duly constituted order: “I have learned the junk equation. Junk is not, like alcohol or weed, a means to increased enjoyment of life. Junk is not a kick. It is a way life” (xvi).<br /><br />Hegel: “His face was lined with suffering in which his eyes did not participate. It was a suffering of his cells alone. He himself—the conscious ego that looked out of the glazed, alert-calm hoodlum eyes—would have nothing to do with this suffering of his rejected other self, a suffering of the nervous system, of flesh and viscera and cells” (3).<br /><br />Junky apocalypse (cf. Selby): “It was as though every doctor in Greater New York has suddenly taken a pledge never to write another narcotics script” (24-25). Given some effect: “Almost worse than the sickness is the depression that goes with it. One afternoon, I closed my eyes and saw New York in ruins. Huge centipedes and scorpions crawled in and out of empty bars and cafeterias and drug stores” (28). <br /><br />Something nifty going on with presence/absence here: “The American upper middle-class citizen is a composite of negatives. He is largely delineated by what he is not. [One guy] was not merely negative. He was positively invisible; a vague respectable presence. There is a certain kind of ghost that can only materialize with the aid of a sheet or other piece of cloth to give it outline” (41-42). Similarly, narrator, while selling narcotics, explains that “I had two caps in my hand and waited for one of the empty spaces you hit in a city” (50). And, addict “was aware of his talent for invisibility, and at times he felt the need for holding himself together so he would at least have enough flesh to put a needle in. At these times he would assemble all his claims to reality” (55).<br /><br />Some New Orleans content: <blockquote>Along Bourbon Street are ruins of the 1920s. Down here the French Quarter blends into Skid Row are ruins of an earlier stratum: chili joints, decaying hotels, oldtime saloons with mahogany bars, spittoons, and crystal chandeliers. The ruins of 1900. There are people in New Orleans who have never been outside the city limits. The New Orleans accent is exactly similar to the accent of Brooklyn. The French Quarter is always crowded. Tourists, servicemen, merchant seamen, gamblers, perverts, drifters, and lamsters from every State of the Union. People wander around, unrelated, purposeless, most of them looking vaguely sullen and hostile. This is a place where you enjoy yourself. Even the criminals have come here to cool off and relax. (68-69) </blockquote> As implied, supra, the main interest here is Agamben’s <i>State of Exception</i> arguments, vis-à-vis the corporeal constitution: <blockquote>It is possible to detach yourself from most pain—injury to teeth, eyes, and genitals present special difficulties—so that the pain is experienced as neutral excitation. From junk sickness there seems to be no escape. Junk sickness is the reverse side of junk kick. The kick of junk is that you have to have it. Junkies run on junk time and junk metabolism. They are subject to junk climate. They are warmed and chilled by junk. The kick of junk is living under junk conditions. You cannot escape from junk sickness any more than you can escape from junk kick after a shot. (97)</blockquote> Junkies “all look alike, as if wearing a costume identical in some curious way that escapes exact tabulation. Junk has marked them all with its indelible brand” (122). The constitutional order of heroin is outside the passage of time--“When you look back over a year on the junk, it seems like no time at all. Only the periods when you were sick stand out” (123)—which suggests that temporal dislocation is agambenian, what is marked out in memory is outside of the constitutional order but necessary for its maintenance.<br /><br />Heroin is “a biological necessity when you have a habit, an invisible mouth” (124) and “takes everything and gives nothing but insurance against junk sickness” (125)—that is, heroin is simultaneously what creates the necessity and insurance against the necessity. It is without doubt one of the best examples of an Agambenian SoE: “Junk is an inoculation of death that keeps the body in a condition of emergency” (127), as well as of Derrida’s pharmakon made manifest: “After ten days of cure I had deteriorated shockingly” (127).<br /><br />Recommended for readers boneless like deep-sea creatures and strangers regarded with distaste by the closed corporation of the desirables.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1609022385892597672" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_21669381" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/21669381" />
    <a title="Chris" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/733629-chris"><img alt="Chris" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1199331851p2/733629.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/21669381?book_show_action=true">May 05, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chris" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chris" href="/user/show/733629-chris">Chris</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140043518" href="/book/show/538566.Junky">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">small-time suckaz</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Chris by: </span>
      the voices that beg for the needle, maaaaan
  </div>


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer21669381" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13606086395336172618">Junkies suck. This may not be ‘breaking news’ to some, but I just had to get that off my chest. Also, the undeniable fact that junkies suck is going to come into play a lot here, so I would guess that if you’re sympathetic to the plight of the many nimrods currently haunted by the specter of addiction, you’re probably not going to give much of a shit about what I have to say. Later. Piss off.<br/><br/>	We haven’t purged all the scourge yet. The ‘beat’ movement sucks too. Oh, you think because a handful of</span>
  <span id="freeText13606086395336172618" style="display:none">Junkies suck. This may not be ‘breaking news’ to some, but I just had to get that off my chest. Also, the undeniable fact that junkies suck is going to come into play a lot here, so I would guess that if you’re sympathetic to the plight of the many nimrods currently haunted by the specter of addiction, you’re probably not going to give much of a shit about what I have to say. Later. Piss off.<br /><br />	We haven’t purged all the scourge yet. The ‘beat’ movement sucks too. Oh, you think because a handful of schmucks proclaimed they were the beacon of brilliance for a generation that it’s an uncontested fact. You actually think these dudes are some hep sh!t? Don’t let the door smack your weak ass on the way out.<br /><br />	Lastly, this isn’t for anyone suffering from any sort of affliction beginning with the letter ‘L’, so you might as well f#ck off too. I don’t care if it is lumbago, leukemia, left-handedness, lyme disease, lupus, a lazy eye, a limp, or a lump on the nuts. You’ve got more important things to do than read this; go apply some ointment or rub some dirt on that sh!t in the hopes of a brighter future.<br /><br />	“Junky” truly sucks. There are about 150 characters briefly introduced in the 152 pages of the book, just random, lame-ass addicts gimping around, getting in sh!t, scoring some sh!t, peddling some sh!t, whatever. Really, that’s about it. “The heat was on so I had to chill in New Orleans for a week. I started copping H from Old Blue, but a wild-eyed, hard-drinkin queer called Brick told me Old Blue was a snitch. I had to shake the law, so I checked into rehab where I met Marcus and Squirting Pete.” Feel free to insert that anywhere within the story; seriously, it would probably go unnoticed. Not a whole hell of a lot happens, certainly nothing interesting, while Bill Burroughs goes around picking up dudes, rolling drunks, and whining about this or that between getting his smack on. Some might say that Burroughs paints a compelling portrait of the colorless and desolate landscape traversed only by the blighted and afflicted. Some might find the growing mania spawned by his simple-minded and obsessive reliance on junk an interesting account of the human condition. Where are those people and why haven’t they been bludgeoned. <br /><br />	I’m giving this book two stars. It deserves one star. I am going to copy the passage in the book which garnered some completely unworthy distinction: <br />	“A room full of fags gives me the horrors. They jerk around like puppets on invisible strings, galvanized into hideous activity that is the negation of everything living and spontaneous. The live human being has moved out of these bodies long ago. But something moved in when the original tenant moved out. Fags are ventriloquists’ dummies who have moved in and taken over the ventriloquist.”<br /><br />	That about sums it up. That and the fact I couldn’t get the thought out of my mind that this guy was an influence on my man Hunter ‘Cannon-Fodder’ Thompson. (see p.127 for something disheartening)<br /><br />	Lest I forget: the fact Burroughs felt the need for a ‘glossary’ is pretty badass….. This tells you several things right there. 1) He obviously thinks the work will be a timeless classic, and that the half-human-half-alien fiends of 2120 will probably need this hip reference. 2) He knows the contemporary reader is not the hardcore junkie he is, and will also require this wisdom. 3) He assumes that he’s somehow responsible (if not the key element) for the success any of these words have in making it part of standard vernacular. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13606086395336172618" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_176851776" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/176851776?book_show_action=true">Jun 15, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Benji" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Benji" href="/user/show/2550185-benji">Benji</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/4517763-junky">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2550185-benji?shelf=2011">2011</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2550185-benji?shelf=creepy">creepy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2550185-benji?shelf=read-in-morocco">read-in-morocco</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2550185-benji?shelf=12-hour-read">12-hour-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2550185-benji?shelf=weak-literary-value">weak-literary-value</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2550185-benji?shelf=weak-excitement-value">weak-excitement-value</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer176851776" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5765471065573498475"><br/>It's very dry. I felt the whole thing was a list of prices and complaints that noone was paying. At the same time, the dryness of it had this normalizing effect that would wear off every 20 or 30 pages. My awareness of the horror of it would thus come in and out. Likewise, the introduction could have been a more-interesting substitute for reading the whole book--I felt like whatever interest the novel had was condensed into those 25 pages. So from then on it was hearing those same things a secon</span>
  <span id="freeText5765471065573498475" style="display:none"><br />It's very dry. I felt the whole thing was a list of prices and complaints that noone was paying. At the same time, the dryness of it had this normalizing effect that would wear off every 20 or 30 pages. My awareness of the horror of it would thus come in and out. Likewise, the introduction could have been a more-interesting substitute for reading the whole book--I felt like whatever interest the novel had was condensed into those 25 pages. So from then on it was hearing those same things a second-time and not much else. But then the man says you have to read it 3 or 4 times in order to get the whole profundity, and I don't have that much interest.<br /><br />I started skimming in and out for the last 30 pages, but that was the best part so then I focused more. When he becomes a little more frantic and seems tohit bottom starts to be the interesting part. The first 100 pages though are like chewing cardboard -- with such a big subject, it's amazing the sheer quantity of interesting points that seem to have been left out. But maybe the point is that, like anything, even heroin can become banal and unremarkable.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5765471065573498475" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1333916771" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1333916771" />
    <a title="Steven Godin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin"><img alt="Steven Godin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605181037p2/26706841.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1333916771?book_show_action=true">Jul 13, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steven Godin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steven Godin" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin">Steven Godin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=memoir-autobiography">memoir-autobiography</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=america-canada">america-canada</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=existentialism">existentialism</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1333916771" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7318973714831313977">A honest,truthful and personal account of the constant struggle with drug addition, set in 40's new york,new orleans and mexico city it was tough going but not as hard hitting as I thought it would be.Not knowing much about this subject I did find it rather interesting and you reallv do feel the desperation of trying to come of junk only to fall prey to it once again, but its difficult to feel much sympathy as this hell hole of a life was chosen and not forced upon and it leaves you in the end w</span>
  <span id="freeText7318973714831313977" style="display:none">A honest,truthful and personal account of the constant struggle with drug addition, set in 40's new york,new orleans and mexico city it was tough going but not as hard hitting as I thought it would be.Not knowing much about this subject I did find it rather interesting and you reallv do feel the desperation of trying to come of junk only to fall prey to it once again, but its difficult to feel much sympathy as this hell hole of a life was chosen and not forced upon and it leaves you in the end with more questions than answers. This was only a short read but did feel longer as everything does get repetitive, but saying that it was an important read more than anything else. </span>
  <a data-text-id="7318973714831313977" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_50518090" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/50518090" />
    <a title="Bunny " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1519823-bunny"><img alt="Bunny " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1550069180p2/1519823.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/50518090?book_show_action=true">Mar 26, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bunny " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bunny " href="/user/show/1519823-bunny">Bunny </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1519823-bunny?shelf=read-in-09">read-in-09</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer50518090" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3822925148765735561">Books about drugs should never be boring. As a rule.<br/><br/>The life of a junkie (as I have always spelled it) is not boring. The effects of the drug, what happens to the lives of the people who get sucked in, who need the high and will do most anything to get it, none of this is boring.<br/><br/>Goddamn, this book bored me to tears. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('/mX0d6orYnrp2+jgyZYqJh4weAbnNLvcVIznBi/hjC7EQ52lEXWrEk7wXIE+YyD5yG+fzim5NbsP8qG34qJ+tA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('qcUHiofUCC7P2kVIq2dGP+V0RTP2TxXj6sACnmtbgoqT425YPIrBRmjx8SlckkzgMyui+zjCm4SxvkQvphhwEA==')})
  }

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/4462369.William_S_Burroughs">About William S. Burroughs</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/4462369.William_S_Burroughs"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1459243207p3/4462369.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/4462369.William_S_Burroughs">William S. Burroughs
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
5,178
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer11488086251408905970">William Seward Burroughs II, (also known by his pen name William Lee; February 5, 1914 – August 2, 1997) was an American novelist, short story writer, essayist, painter, and spoken word performer. A primary figure of the Beat Generation and a major postmodernist author, he is considered to be "one of the most politically trenchant, culturally influential, and innovative artists of the 20th century</span>
  <span id="freeText11488086251408905970" style="display:none">William Seward Burroughs II, (also known by his pen name William Lee; February 5, 1914 – August 2, 1997) was an American novelist, short story writer, essayist, painter, and spoken word performer. A primary figure of the Beat Generation and a major postmodernist author, he is considered to be "one of the most politically trenchant, culturally influential, and innovative artists of the 20th century". His influence is considered to have affected a range of popular culture as well as literature. Burroughs wrote 18 novels and novellas, six collections of short stories and four collections of essays. Five books have been published of his interviews and correspondences. He also collaborated on projects and recordings with numerous performers and musicians, and made many appearances in films.<br />He was born to a wealthy family in St. Louis, Missouri, grandson of the inventor and founder of the Burroughs Corporation, William Seward Burroughs I, and nephew of public relations manager Ivy Lee. Burroughs began writing essays and journals in early adolescence. He left home in 1932 to attend Harvard University, studied English, and anthropology as a postgraduate, and later attended medical school in Vienna. After being turned down by the Office of Strategic Services and U.S. Navy in 1942 to serve in World War II, he dropped out and became afflicted with the drug addiction that affected him for the rest of his life, while working a variety of jobs. In 1943 while living in New York City, he befriended Allen Ginsberg and Jack Kerouac, the mutually influential foundation of what became the countercultural movement of the Beat Generation.<br />Much of Burroughs's work is semi-autobiographical, primarily drawn from his experiences as a heroin addict, as he lived throughout Mexico City, London, Paris, Berlin, the South American Amazon and Tangier in Morocco. Finding success with his confessional first novel, Junkie (1953), Burroughs is perhaps best known for his third novel Naked Lunch (1959), a controversy-fraught work that underwent a court case under the U.S. sodomy laws. With Brion Gysin, he also popularized the literary cut-up technique in works such as The Nova Trilogy (1961–64). In 1983, Burroughs was elected to the American Academy and Institute of Arts and Letters, and in 1984 was awarded the Ordre des Arts et des Lettres by France. Jack Kerouac called Burroughs the "greatest satirical writer since Jonathan Swift", a reputation he owes to his "lifelong subversion" of the moral, political and economic systems of modern American society, articulated in often darkly humorous sardonicism. J. G. Ballard considered Burroughs to be "the most important writer to emerge since the Second World War", while Norman Mailer declared him "the only American writer who may be conceivably possessed by genius".<br />Burroughs had one child, William Seward Burroughs III (1947-1981), with his second wife Joan Vollmer. Vollmer died in 1951 in Mexico City. Burroughs was convicted of manslaughter in Vollmer's death, an event that deeply permeated all of his writings. Burroughs died at his home in Lawrence, Kansas, after suffering a heart attack in 1997.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11488086251408905970" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/24861-junky">Quotes from Junky</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;When you stop growing you start dying.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/314286-when-you-stop-growing-you-start-dying">397 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;The question is frequently asked: Why does a man become a drug addict?<br />The answer is that he usually does not intend to become an addict. You don’t wake up one morning and decide to be a drug addict. It takes at least three months’ shooting twice a day to get any habit at all. And you don’t really know what junk sickness is until you have had several habits. It took me almost six months to get my first habit, and then the withdrawal symptoms were mild. I think it no exaggeration to say it takes about a year and several hundred injections to make an addict.<br />The questions, of course, could be asked: Why did you ever try narcotics? Why did you continue using it long enough to become an addict? You become a narcotics addict because you do not have strong motivations in the other direction. Junk wins by default. I tried it as a matter of curiosity. I drifted along taking shots when I could score. I ended up hooked. Most addicts I have talked to report a similar experience. They did not start using drugs for any reason they can remember. They just drifted along until they got hooked. If you have never been addicted, you can have no clear idea what it means to need junk with the addict’s special need. You don’t decide to be an addict. One morning you wake up sick and you’re an addict. (Junky, Prologue, p. xxxviii)&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
